## Domain Knowledge
**Domain**: chemical reaction kinetics (rate laws / kinetic ODE modeling)
**Analysis**: The target dA_dt is explicitly the time-derivative of a concentration A(t), which is the standard setup of chemical kinetics: an ODE rate law relating dA_dt to A (and sometimes explicit time t via forcing, deactivation, or changing conditions). With only t and A available, the most likely models are elementary-order consumption/production terms, reversible/source terms, and saturation or inhibition-type rate expressions.

**Common formula structures** (built-in generic + LLM domain-specific):
- Arrhenius equation: k = c0 * exp(c1 / T) (chemical reaction rate dependence on temperature)
- Coulomb's / Newton's inverse-square law: F = c0 / r**2 (gravitational or electrostatic force)
- Stefan-Boltzmann law: P = c0 * T**4 (blackbody radiation power vs temperature)
- Beer-Lambert law: I = c0 * exp(c1 * x) (light intensity attenuation through a medium)
- Michaelis-Menten kinetics: v = c0 * S / (S + c1) (enzyme-catalyzed reaction saturation)
- Hill equation: y = c0 * x**c1 / (x**c1 + c2) (cooperative binding, sigmoidal dose-response)
- Kepler's third law: T**2 = c0 * a**3 (orbital period vs semi-major axis)
- Newton's law of cooling: T = c0 + c1 * exp(c2 * t) (exponential thermal relaxation)
- Clausius-Clapeyron relation: log(P) = c0 + c1 / T (vapor pressure vs temperature)
- Langmuir adsorption isotherm: theta = c0 * P / (1 + c1 * P) (surface adsorption equilibrium)
- Freundlich isotherm: q = c0 * C**c1 (empirical adsorption on heterogeneous surface)
- Stokes' drag: F = c0 * r * v (viscous drag force on a sphere in fluid)
- Logistic growth: N = c0 / (1 + c1 * exp(c2 * t)) (population dynamics with carrying capacity)
- Planck-Wien approximation: I = c0 * f**3 * exp(c1 * f / T) (thermal radiation spectrum)
- Zero-order consumption: dA_dt = -c0 (constant-rate depletion; A decreases linearly in t)
- Zero-order production (source): dA_dt = c0 (constant feed/generation of A)
- First-order decay: dA_dt = -c0 * A (unimolecular decay; exponential A(t))
- Linear birth–death form: dA_dt = c0 + c1 * A (net source plus first-order loss/gain; common near steady-state linearization)
- Second-order consumption: dA_dt = -c0 * A**2 (bimolecular 2A→ products; hyperbolic-in-time integrated behavior)
- Nth-order power-law rate: dA_dt = -c0 * A**c1 (empirical/fractional reaction order fits)
- Reversible/relaxation-to-equilibrium (affine): dA_dt = -c0 * A + c1 (approach to equilibrium A_eq = c1 * (c0)**(-1) in the classic form)
- Quadratic autocatalytic / self-limiting: dA_dt = c0 * A - c1 * A**2 (net growth at low A, saturation at higher A; common for autocatalysis + depletion)
- Logistic-like autocatalysis toward limiting concentration: dA_dt = c0 * A * (c1 - A) (A grows/decays toward an upper limit c1 depending on sign and initial A)
- Inhibition / saturation in effective rate constant: dA_dt = -c0 * A / (1 + c1 * A) (self-inhibition or limited catalyst/active sites; transitions from ~-c0*A to ~-(c0/c1))
- Time-decaying activity (catalyst deactivation surrogate): dA_dt = -c0 * exp(-c1 * t) * A (effective first-order rate decreases over time)
- Time-dependent forcing/input: dA_dt = -c0 * A + c1 * exp(-c2 * t) (consumption with a transient feed/impulse-like source)
- Mixed-order (parallel pathways): dA_dt = -c0 * A - c1 * A**2 (simultaneous first- and second-order loss channels)
- Low-order polynomial kinetics (Taylor surrogate): dA_dt = c0 + c1 * A + c2 * A**2 (phenomenological fit around operating regime)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- Pure consumption of A typically implies dA_dt < 0 for A > 0; models often enforce this via leading negative coefficients multiplying functions of A.
- If dA_dt is proportional to A (dA_dt ≈ -c * A), then A(t) is exponential in time; deviations from exponential suggest higher-order terms or time-varying coefficients.
- Curvature of dA_dt vs A on a log–log plot indicates reaction order: slope ≈ 1 implies first-order, slope ≈ 2 implies second-order, non-integer slope suggests fractional/empirical order.
- A constant offset term in dA_dt (e.g., +c0) usually corresponds to a continuous source/sink (feed, baseline generation, or an unmodeled reservoir).
- An affine relaxation form dA_dt = -c0 * A + c1 indicates convergence to a nonzero steady state; the sign/magnitude of c1 sets the equilibrium level and can represent reversibility or constant replenishment.
- A negative quadratic term (e.g., -c * A**2) often indicates bimolecular loss (2A or A+something approximated as proportional to A), which makes depletion accelerate at higher A.
- A combination like c0*A - c1*A**2 suggests autocatalysis with self-limitation (net growth at low A, saturation/decline at high A), producing an interior steady state at A ≈ c0 * (c1)**(-1) in the classic form.
- Rational inhibition forms such as A/(1 + c*A) imply a transition from first-order behavior at low A to a rate that becomes less sensitive to A at high A (site-limitation, crowding, or inhibitor buildup).
- Explicit dependence on t (e.g., exp(-c*t) factors) is a hallmark of changing conditions: catalyst deactivation, temperature ramps approximated in time, or transient dosing/pulses.

So any matching candidate must be able to produce **(i)** exactly one term of the form \(A\exp(k t)\) with a **negative** coefficient and \(k=-0.0816...\), and **(ii)** exactly a \(\cos(\log(A+1))\) term with amplitude \(0.84034...\), and **(iii)** no extra \(A\)-only, \(t\)-only, rational-in-\(A\), \(\log\)-in-\(A\), \(\exp(A\cdot)\), \(\sin(\cdot)\), power terms, etc., unless they can be identically zeroed out by parameter choices.

A key structural obstacle: among all candidates, **none contains a cosine of a logarithm** (no \(\cos(\log(\cdot))\) anywhere). Since \(\cos(\log(A+1))\) is not representable for all \(A\) by combinations of plain logs, exponentials in \(A\), rationals, polynomials, or sines with linear arguments, every candidate will fail on term (ii). GT Equivalence Verification (CRK7)
============================================================
GT: -c0*A*exp(-c1*_s*t) + c2*_w*cos(log(A + c3))
Candidates evaluated: 50
Matched candidate IDs: []
Matched candidates: []

I need to determine which candidates can exactly match the ground truth:

**GT:** `-0.731770*A*exp(-0.08162*t) + 0.84034*cos(log(A + 1))`

The GT has two terms:
1. `A*exp(c*t)` — an exponential decay in t multiplied by A
2. `cos(log(A+1))` — a cosine of a logarithm

The key challenge is: **can `cos(log(A+1))` be represented by the basis functions available in each candidate?**

The function `cos(log(A+1))` is a very specific transcendental function. Let me check what basis functions candidates use to approximate the A-dependent non-exponential-decay part:

**Common basis functions in candidates:**
- `log(A*c + 1)` — logarithmic
- `exp(A*c)` — exponential in A
- `A/(A+c)` or `A*c/(A+c)` or `c/(A+c)` — rational
- `A^n` — polynomial/power
- `sqrt(A)`, `A^(1/3)` — fractional powers
- `sin(A*c + c)` — sinusoidal in A

**Critical question:** Can `cos(log(A+1))` be exactly represented by any finite combination of `log`, `exp`, polynomials, rational functions, or `sin` terms?

`cos(log(A+1))` is NOT a polynomial, NOT a rational function, NOT an exponential, NOT a logarithm, and NOT a finite sum of any of these. These are linearly independent function classes.

**What about `sin(A*c + c)` terms?** Note that `cos(log(A+1))` involves a sinusoidal function of `log(A+1)`, not of `A` itself. `sin(c*A + c)` is sinusoidal in A (linear argument), while `cos(log(A+1))` is sinusoidal in `log(A+1)` (logarithmic argument). These are fundamentally different functions and cannot be made equal for all A.

Let me verify: Could `sin(c*A + c)` equal `cos(log(A+1))` for all A≥0? No — `sin(c*A+c)` is periodic in A with period `2π/c`, while `cos(log(A+1))` is not periodic in A. They are structurally different.

Similarly, sums of `log(A*c+1)` terms, `exp(A*c)` terms, rational functions, and power functions cannot reproduce `cos(log(A+1))` exactly. These are all monotonic or have fundamentally different oscillatory behavior.

**Conclusion:** None of the candidates contain `cos(log(...))` or any equivalent basis function. The GT's `cos(log(A+1))` term cannot be exactly represented by any combination of the basis functions available in any candidate.

Let me verify each candidate systematically — they all use combinations of: `A*exp(c*t)`, `log(A*c+1)`, `exp(A*c)`, `A/(A+c)`, `A^p`, `sqrt(A)`, `sin(A*c+c)`, `1/A^p`, polynomials in A and t. None include `cos(log(...))`.

Since `cos(log(A+1))` cannot be exactly represented by any of these basis functions (by linear independence of transcendental function families), no candidate can match.

Candidate 1 through 50 verdict: [NO MATCH] for all.
